            }
            return "blank";
        }

    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="eea1629e1cae401bbba43227f93b488d">

        function getParameter(parameterName) {
            let result = null,
                tmp = [];
            location.search
                .substr(1)
                .split("&")
                .forEach(function (item) {
                    tmp = item.split("=");
                    if (tmp[0] === parameterName) result = tmp[1];
                });
            return result;
        }

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            queryParamsDelimiter: encodeURIComponent('?'),

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                let redirectURL = getParameter('redirectUri');
                if(redirectURL == null){
                    redirectURL = encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
                }
                if(redirectURL.indexOf('saml_referrer') < 0){
                    redirectURL += encodeURIComponent(((redirectURL.indexOf(this.queryParamsDelimiter) !== -1) ? '&' : '?') + 'saml_referrer');
                }
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + redirectURL;
            },
            retrieveEntity: function () {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // get the results
                            var results = res.data;
                            return resolve(results);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('retrieve entity error: ' + err);
                        });
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage duo to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {doi: doi, entityid: entityId, entityTitle: entityTitle, eventGroupKey: '6dd079fe-e5bc-4164-a7cb-263aba993dc4'}

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (let i in allSsoInstitutions) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();
                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err){
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

        <div class="relative"><a href="#" data-db-target-for="0e406ce9-5c43-4e2d-b741-e0b43d1f1865" data-db-switch="icon-close_thin" aria-haspopup="true" aria-controls="0e406ce9-5c43-4e2d-b741-e0b43d1f1865_Pop" role="button" id="0e406ce9-5c43-4e2d-b741-e0b43d1f1865_Ctrl" aria-label="Tools" class="article-tools__ctrl"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Tools"></i><span>Tools</span></a><div data-db-target-of="0e406ce9-5c43-4e2d-b741-e0b43d1f1865" aria-labelledby="0e406ce9-5c43-4e2d-b741-e0b43d1f1865_Ctrl" id="0e406ce9-5c43-4e2d-b741-e0b43d1f1865_Pop" class="article-tools__block fixed dropBlock__holder"><ul role="menu" class="rlist w-slide--list"><li role="presentation" class="article-tool"><a href="https://onlinelibrary.wiley.com/action/rightsLink?doi=10.1002%2Fanie.202417779&amp;mode=" role="menuitem" target="_blank"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-permissions-key"></i><span>Request permission</span></a></li><li role="presentation" class="article-tool"><a href="/action/showCitFormats?doi=10.1002%2Fanie.202417779" role="menuitem" target="_self"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-export-icon"></i><span>Export citation</span></a></li><li role="presentation" class="article-tool"><a href="/personalize/addFavoritePublication?doi=10.1002%2Fanie.202417779" role="menuitem" target="_self"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-favorites"></i><span>Add to favorites</span></a></li><li role="presentation" class="article-tool"><a href="/action/addCitationAlert?doi=10.1002%2Fanie.202417779" role="menuitem" target="_self"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Track-citations"></i><span>Track citation</span></a></li></ul></div></div>

   <div class="abstract-group ">
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" id="abstract-graphical-en">
         <h2 id="d242769908" class="article-section__header section__title graphical abstractlang_en graphical">Graphical Abstract</h2>
         <div class="graphical-abstract">
            <p>Azatriangulenetrione unit is first incorporated into COF backbone to form a high-performance COF electrode with intrinsic multiple reachable redox-active sites of C=O, C=N and C=C, which delivers a superb discharge capacity of 410.4 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.1 A g<sup>−1</sup> in SIBs.
            </p><figure>
               <div class="center graphic__image"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/32eda15c-ae2c-44b9-a12a-73a41fbf4081/anie202417779-toc-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/32eda15c-ae2c-44b9-a12a-73a41fbf4081/anie202417779-toc-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/463a6a00-70c8-4852-a2bb-2f1b9af8ec73/anie202417779-toc-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/32eda15c-ae2c-44b9-a12a-73a41fbf4081/anie202417779-toc-0001-m.jpg" alt="Description unavailable" title="Description unavailable" loading="lazy"></picture></a><p class="caption-style"></p>
               </div>
            </figure>
            <p></p>
            </div>
      </section>
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en">
         <h2 id="d242769941" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            
            <p>Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) suffer from sluggish kinetics, large volume change, and limited specific capacity due to the large radius of Na<sup>+</sup>. These issues can be solved through using covalent organic frameworks (COFs) as electrodes. Herein, an azatriangulenetrione-containing COF (denoted as CityU-33) was designed and synthesized as an electrode material for SIBs. Due to its inherent abundance of multiple redox-active sites and fast intercalation kinetics, CityU-33 delivered a high discharge capacity of 410.4 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.1 A g<sup>−1</sup> and showed remarkable long-term cycling stability, where a discharge capacity of 288 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.2 A g<sup>−1</sup> with 97 % retention over 2000 cycles was achieved, making it the top COF electrode material for SIBs.</p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="anie202417779-sec-0001">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="anie202417779-sec-0001-title"> Introduction</h2>
         
         <p>The realization of a carbon–neutral economy highly depends on the advancements in renewable energy technologies.<span><sup><a href="#anie202417779-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202417779-bib-0001_R_d242769784e674" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1">1</a></sup></span> Among these technologies, lithium-ion batteries (LIBs) have shown their advantages in rechargeable battery markets owing to their high energy density and long working lifespan.<span><sup><a href="#anie202417779-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202417779-bib-0005_R_d242769784e677" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 2">2</a></sup></span> However, the scarcity of lithium and its uneven global distribution have led to high market volatility and potential threats to the supply chain.<span><sup><a href="#anie202417779-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202417779-bib-0008_R_d242769784e680" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3">3</a></sup></span> Thus, searching for alternatives to replace LIBs is necessary and important.</p>
         
         <p>Because the storage principle of SIBs is similar to that of LIBs and sodium is naturally abundant and low cost, SIBs have become a practical option.<span><sup><a href="#anie202417779-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202417779-bib-0011_R_d242769784e686" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4">4</a></sup></span> However, the regularly used anodic material graphite in LIBs has poor performance in SIBs because the reversibly electrochemical intercalation of Na<sup>+</sup> into graphite layers is more difficult considering the much larger ionic radius of sodium compared with that of lithium.<span><sup><a href="#anie202417779-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202417779-bib-0016_R_d242769784e691" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5">5</a></sup></span> Besides, the theoretical specific capacity of storing Na<sup>+</sup> in graphite is only 31 mAh ⋅ g<sup>−1</sup> through forming a thermodynamically unstable NaC<sub>70</sub>.<span><sup><a href="#anie202417779-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202417779-bib-0019_R_d242769784e701" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - Solid State Ionics">6</a></sup></span> In fact, anodic materials is one of key factors to determine the energy density of battery devices as well as their lifetime and rate capability.<span><sup><a href="#anie202417779-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202417779-bib-0020_R_d242769784e704" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7">7</a></sup></span> Therefore, developing high-performance anode materials is highly desirable.</p>
         
         <p>Various metal oxides,<span><sup><a href="#anie202417779-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202417779-bib-0024_R_d242769784e710" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - Acc. Chem. Res.">8</a></sup></span> alloys,<span><sup><a href="#anie202417779-bib-0025" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202417779-bib-0025_R_d242769784e713" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9">9</a></sup></span> and phosphorus,<span><sup><a href="#anie202417779-bib-0028" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202417779-bib-0028_R_d242769784e716" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - Small Methods">10</a></sup></span> have been investigated as anodes and shown high capacity in SIBs. However, these materials suffer from the issues of low conductivity, huge volume expansion, and pulverization.<span><sup><a href="#anie202417779-bib-0029" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202417779-bib-0029_R_d242769784e719" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - J. Mater. Chem. A">11</a></sup></span> Non-graphite carbonaceous anodes were also developed to improve the performance of SIBs. In particular, hard carbon, which was first reported by Stevens and co-workers,<span><sup><a href="#anie202417779-bib-0030" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202417779-bib-0030_R_d242769784e722" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - J. Electrochem. Soc.">12</a></sup></span> stands out due to the merits in cost, structural diversity and scalable synthesis, as well as safety.<span><sup><a href="#anie202417779-bib-0031" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202417779-bib-0031_R_d242769784e726" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13">13</a></sup></span> More importantly, the nanopores not only facilitate the migration of electrons and ions but provide a buffer zone for the compensation of large volume change during the redox reactions.<span><sup><a href="#anie202417779-bib-0034" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202417779-bib-0034_R_d242769784e729" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - Nat. Commun.">14</a></sup></span> Unfortunately, their disordered structures make sodium storage mechanism elusive and debatable.<span><sup><a href="#anie202417779-bib-0035" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202417779-bib-0035_R_d242769784e732" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - Molecules">15</a></sup></span> Besides, they generally delivered a low initial Coulombic efficiency and cycle performance, which significantly hampered their practical application in SIBs.<span><sup><a href="#anie202417779-bib-0036" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202417779-bib-0036_R_d242769784e735" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - J. Energy Storage">16</a></sup></span> To address these issues, covalent organic frameworks (COFs) might be a good choice because porous COFs with tunable redox centers can endow SIBs with high capacity, fast intercalation kinetics and less volume changes.</p>
         
         <p>COFs are a class of crystalline polymers constructed from small building blocks via covalent bonds,<span><sup><a href="#anie202417779-bib-0037" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202417779-bib-0037_R_d242769784e741" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17">17</a></sup></span> where the density of reversible redox-active units such as C=C,<span><sup><a href="#anie202417779-bib-0048" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202417779-bib-0048_R_d242769784e744" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - CCS Chemistry">18</a></sup></span> C=O,<span><sup><a href="#anie202417779-bib-0049" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202417779-bib-0049_R_d242769784e747" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19">19</a></sup></span> C=N,<span><sup><a href="#anie202417779-bib-0051" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202417779-bib-0051_R_d242769784e750" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19b - Nat. Commun.">19b</a>-<a href="#anie202417779-bib-0053" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202417779-bib-0053_R_d242769784e750" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19d - Chem. Mater.">19d</a>, <a href="#anie202417779-bib-0055" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202417779-bib-0055_R_d242769784e750" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20">20</a></sup></span> and N=N<span><sup><a href="#anie202417779-bib-0058" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202417779-bib-0058_R_d242769784e753" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - Nanoscale Horiz.">21</a></sup></span> are necessary to contribute to the high capacity of energy storage.<span><sup><a href="#anie202417779-bib-0059" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202417779-bib-0059_R_d242769784e757" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">22</a></sup></span> The periodically extended structures with defined redox centers ensure the redox mechanism understandable and facilitate the establishment of structure-properties-performance relationships during the materials design and optimization. Despite several pioneering works,<span><sup><a href="#anie202417779-bib-0060" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202417779-bib-0060_R_d242769784e760" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - Prog. Nat. Sci. Mater. Int.">23</a></sup></span> inevitable defects of either unsatisfactory capacity or low crystallinity in reported COF electrodes hampered the maximal use of COF merits in SIBs. In fact, the well-ordered COF materials featuring uniform open nano-porosities and multiple redox centers can not only accelerate the transport of Na<sup>+</sup> during the redox process but also provide sufficient space for their accommodation, thereby benefiting the fast kinetics, free of volume expansions and high capacity.</p>
         
         <p>Different from traditional building units for the construction of COFs, azatriangulenetrione is a versatile core with multiple redox sites that has been demonstrated to show many applications in various areas such as enhancing long-distance energy migration,<span><sup><a href="#anie202417779-bib-0061" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202417779-bib-0061_R_d242769784e769" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24">24</a></sup></span> promoting chiral self-assembly,<span><sup><a href="#anie202417779-bib-0064" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202417779-bib-0064_R_d242769784e772" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 25">25</a></sup></span> and tuning emissive properties.<span><sup><a href="#anie202417779-bib-0067" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202417779-bib-0067_R_d242769784e775" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 26 - Angew. Chem. Int. Ed.">26</a></sup></span> However, employing this electroactive unit to construct COFs for energy storage is rarely reported. This gap strongly encourages us to synthesize a benzophenone-imine protected azatriangulenetrione precursor (ATTO-3N) for preparation of an azatriangulenetrione-contained COF (denoted as CityU-33) after reacting with 4,4′,4′′-(1,3,5-triazine-2,4,6-triyl)tribenzaldehyde (TFPT). Used as an anodic electrode in SIBs, CityU-33 displayed excellent performance, with a superb discharge capacity of 410.4 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.1 A g<sup>−1</sup> in Na//CityU-33 cell. The in situ/ex situ Fourier transform infrared spectroscopies (FTIR), X-ray photoelectron spectroscopies (XPS) and theoretical calculations reveal that there are a total of 12 Na<sup>+</sup> storage sites, including C=O, C=N, and C=C in one repeated unit of CityU-33. Moreover, a discharge capacity of 288 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at a low current of 0.2 A g<sup>−1</sup> with approximately 97 % retention over 2000 cycles was also demonstrated, indicating its good cyclability.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="anie202417779-sec-0002">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="anie202417779-sec-0002-title"> Results and Discussion</h2>
         
         <p>Although azatriangulenetrione has been demonstrated as a key scaffold for advanced photoelectronic applications,<span><sup><a href="#anie202417779-bib-0061" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202417779-bib-0061_R_d242769784e798" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24">24</a>, <a href="#anie202417779-bib-0064" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202417779-bib-0064_R_d242769784e798" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 25">25</a></sup></span> details related to the preparation of amino-functionalized precursors are still limited.<span><sup><a href="#anie202417779-bib-0068" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202417779-bib-0068_R_d242769784e801" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 27 - Chemistry">27</a></sup></span> Given the extensive conjugation and planarity of azatriangulenetrione moiety,<span><sup><a href="#anie202417779-bib-0062" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202417779-bib-0062_R_d242769784e804" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24a - Nature">24a</a></sup></span> we herein adopted a benzophenone-imine protected strategy to overcome the issue of poor solubility.<span><sup><a href="#anie202417779-bib-0069" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202417779-bib-0069_R_d242769784e807" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 28 - Mater. Horiz.">28</a></sup></span> The monomer 2,6,10-tris-((diphenylmethylene)amino)-4<i>H</i>,8<i>H</i>,12<i>H</i>-benzo[1,9]quinolizino-[3,4,5,6,7-<i>defg</i>]-acridine-4,8,12-trione (ATTO-3N) can be readily obtained through a Pd-catalyzed amination reaction between benzophenone imine and intermediate of 2,6,10-triiodo-4<i>H</i>,8<i>H</i>,12<i>H</i>-benzo[1,9]quinolizino[3,4,5,6,7-<i>defg</i>]acridine-4,8,12-trione (ATTO-3I), which was synthesized according to the previously reported procedures with minor modification (Scheme S1).<span><sup><a href="#anie202417779-bib-0070" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202417779-bib-0070_R_d242769784e828" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 29 - Tetrahedron">29</a></sup></span> Importantly, the protected strategy remarkably simplified the isolation procedure of ATTO-3N, namely, a simple filtration and washing procedure (Supporting Information, Synthesis). The structure of the as-prepared ATTO-3N was determined by single crystal X-ray diffraction (Scheme <a href="#anie202417779-fig-5001">1</a>: Inserted picture, upper right), and the high purity was confirmed by <sup>1</sup>H- and <sup>13</sup>C-nuclear magnetic resonance (Figure S2 and S3). CityU-33 was synthesized through a solvothermal reaction between ATTO-3N and TFPT in a mixture of 1,2-dichlorobenzene/<i>n</i>-butanol (1/9, <i>v/v</i>) under 150 °C for 3 days (Scheme <a href="#anie202417779-fig-5001">1</a>). The as-formed reddish-brown powder was further treated with Soxhlet extraction to eradicate any possible soluble by-products, then activated under a vacuum at 120 °C for 12 hours to remove the guest molecules before further characterization and application.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="anie202417779-fig-5001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/05187ff7-937b-4cfa-b48f-2503a649d016/anie202417779-fig-5001-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/05187ff7-937b-4cfa-b48f-2503a649d016/anie202417779-fig-5001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/e9d0cad7-e90f-452a-9a37-88819ec02044/anie202417779-fig-5001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/05187ff7-937b-4cfa-b48f-2503a649d016/anie202417779-fig-5001-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Scheme 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=anie202417779-fig-5001&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fanie.202417779" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                     <p>The synthesis of CityU-33. (Inserted: Crystallographic structure of ATTO-3N (upper right), the hydrogen atoms and solvated dichloromethane are omitted for clarity.)</p></div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>The structure of CityU-33 was confirmed by FTIR (Figures <a href="#anie202417779-fig-0001">1a</a> and S4). After the polycondensation, the signal at 1690 cm<sup>−1</sup> belonging to the stretching vibration of aldehyde groups in TFPT disappeared, indicating the full conversion of monomers. The prominent absorption bands belonging to C=O at 1650 cm<sup>−1</sup> and C=N in triazine rings at 1509 cm<sup>−1</sup> were retained. The remarkable augmentation of the vibration at 1625 cm<sup>−1</sup> ascribed to C=N after the polymerization clearly manifested the successful formation of imine linkages in CityU-33. Moreover, XPS provided further support in the successful construction of CityU-33 (Figure S5a). In high-resolution spectra, the deconvoluted C 1s XPS spectra displayed four peaks at 284.7, 285.4, 286.3, and 288.7 eV (Figure <a href="#anie202417779-fig-0001">1b</a>), which can be assigned to the aromatic C=C, C=N, C−N, and C=O, respectively.<span><sup><a href="#anie202417779-bib-0071" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202417779-bib-0071_R_d242769784e885" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 30 - Angew. Chem. Int. Ed.">30</a></sup></span> Peaks with binding energy at 398.9, 400.5, and 401.1 eV in N 1s spectra can be assigned to C=N in imine and triazine, and C−N, respectively (Figure <a href="#anie202417779-fig-0001">1c</a>).<span><sup><a href="#anie202417779-bib-0072" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202417779-bib-0072_R_d242769784e891" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 31 - Angew. Chem. Int. Ed.">31</a></sup></span> Besides, peaks corresponding to the C=O groups can also be observed in O 1s spectra (Figure S5b).
</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="anie202417779-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/13160198-0680-487c-b4c3-864210f44fd0/anie202417779-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/13160198-0680-487c-b4c3-864210f44fd0/anie202417779-fig-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/eb303225-c498-4c24-81b8-d27291fd56cc/anie202417779-fig-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/13160198-0680-487c-b4c3-864210f44fd0/anie202417779-fig-0001-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=anie202417779-fig-0001&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fanie.202417779" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                     <p>FTIR spectra of CityU-33 (a). XPS profiles of CityU-33: (b) C 1s; (c) N 1s. (d) PXRD patterns of experimental (Wine), Pawley-refined (black), simulated stacking (violet), and their difference (light blue), and Bragg positions (green) for CityU-33. (e) Space-filling modes of CityU-33 with layers arranged in an AA stacking mode along c-direction (left) and b-direction (right). (f) Nitrogen adsorption-desorption isotherms of CityU-33 at 77 K (Inserted: pore size distribution).</p></div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>Scanning electron microscopy (SEM) image indicates that CityU-33 are regular rod-like crystallites with a length up to a few micrometers (Figure S6). The crystallinity of CityU-33 was determined by powder X-ray diffraction (PXRD) patterns. Due to the protected effect of benzophenone–imine, the crystallinity of resulting CityU-33 was greatly enhanced.<sup>[28, 32]</sup> Notably, several distinct diffraction peaks located at 4.39°, 7.71°, 8.97° and 11.69° assigning to the (100), (110), (200) and (120) facets, respectively, can be observed and match well with the indexes in calculated AA stacking mode (Figures <a href="#anie202417779-fig-0001">1d</a>, <a href="#anie202417779-fig-0001">1e</a> and Figure S7, S8). In addition, due to the good planar feature of aromatic precursors (ATTO-3N and TFPT), the (001) signal attributing to the strong π–π stacking effect was also discerned. The Pawley refinement was performed to unravel the lattice parameters using the Materials Studio software package. The refined PXRD patterns fitted well to those in experiments with satisfactory convergence residuals (R<sub>wp</sub>=3.79 % and R<sub>p</sub>=2.57 %) (Figure <a href="#anie202417779-fig-0001">1d</a>). The optimized unit parameters for CityU-33 are <i>a</i>=<i>b</i>=23.58 Å, <i>c</i>=3.44 Å (α=90°, β=90°, γ=120°) in the space group P1. Thermal stability of CityU-33 was evaluated by thermogravimetric analysis (TGA), which showed a decomposition temperature of up 300 °C with a weight loss of 5 % under N<sub>2</sub> atmosphere (Figure S9). The permanent porosities of CityU-33 were carried out through nitrogen adsorption/desorption measurements under 77 K. As shown in Figure <a href="#anie202417779-fig-0001">1f</a>, CityU-33 showed a typical type-I reversible isotherm with a large calculated Brunauer–Emmett–Teller (BET) surface areas of 1253.9 m<sup>2</sup> g<sup>−1</sup>, further corroborating the highly ordered porous structure of CityU-33. The size distribution of pores was calculated according to the corresponding N<sub>2</sub> adsorption isotherm using nonlocal density functional theory (NLDFT), and the main micropores were in the range of 1–1.5 nm. The rich accessible redox-active sites, high crystallinity, and uniform porous channels endow CityU-33 promising applications in energy storage fields.</p>
         
         <p>The electrochemical performance of CityU-33 as an anode was studied through the standard coin-type cell with sodium foil as the counter electrode, 1 M NaPF<sub>6</sub> in diethylene glycol dimethyl ether (DEGDME) that is adsorbed with a glass fiber separator was used as the electrolyte. The cyclic voltammetry (CV) curves at various scan rates, ranging from 0.1 to 0.5 mV s<sup>−1</sup>, were examined to explore the reaction kinetics of the CityU-33 electrode, as depicted in Figure <a href="#anie202417779-fig-0002">2a</a>. All curves exhibit four pairs of redox peaks, indicating a four-step Na<sup>+</sup>-storage process. Along with the increasing scan rate, the peak shapes appear to be almost the same, indicating low polarization potentials between cathodic peaks and anodic peaks. The correlation between scan rate (<i>v</i>) and peak current (<i>i)</i> can be expressed as <i>i</i>=a<i>v</i><sup>b</sup>, where a and b are parameters. A b value of 1 indicates that the reaction process is dominated by the capacitance behavior, while a b value of 0.5 signifies that the electrochemical behavior is governed by ionic diffusion. The corresponding log <i>i</i> versus log <i>v</i> plot of the CityU-33 electrode is displayed in Figure <a href="#anie202417779-fig-0002">2b</a>. The calculated b values for peaks 1, 2, 3, 4, 5, 6, 7 and 8 are 0.86, 0.88, 0.98, 0.97, 0.92, 1.0, 1.0 and 1.0, respectively. It is evident that the electrochemical reaction of the CityU-33 electrode is predominantly controlled by capacitance behavior, suggesting rapid kinetics. This phenomenon is ascribed to the infinite diffusion of ions in the aligned channels and the high reactivity of the active sites in CityU-33.<span><sup><a href="#anie202417779-bib-0071" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202417779-bib-0071_R_d242769784e984" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 30 - Angew. Chem. Int. Ed.">30</a></sup></span> As shown in Figure <a href="#anie202417779-fig-0002">2c</a>, the capacitive contribution increases from 67.8 to 90.6 % as the scan rate increases from 0.1 to 0.5 mV s<sup>−1</sup>, suggesting that pseudocapacitors play a crucial role in the discharge-charge processes. The above results reveal a rapid pseudocapacitance process of Na<sup>+</sup> storage in the Na//CityU-33 battery system. The electrochemical impedance spectra (EIS) measurements were performed before and after 500 and 1000 cycles for CityU-33 electrode (Figure S10). Obviously, the charge transfer resistance (<i>R</i><sub>ct</sub>) value of CityU-33 electrode sharply decreases with the increase of the number of cycles. This result reveals that CityU-33 electrode possesses a rapid charge transport ability. It is worth noting that there is an obvious anodic peak around 1.0 V during the first anodic sweep (Figure S11), which is attributed to the formation of solid electrolyte interphase (SEI) on the electrode surface. It can also be reflected by the discharge-charge curves in first cycle under a current density of 0.1 A g<sup>−1</sup> (Figure S12).
</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="anie202417779-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/b82ac518-b88a-4445-a6f7-d58a3ee24a6d/anie202417779-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/b82ac518-b88a-4445-a6f7-d58a3ee24a6d/anie202417779-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/9ed9e2d3-62d2-426e-93d5-36f3677a919a/anie202417779-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/b82ac518-b88a-4445-a6f7-d58a3ee24a6d/anie202417779-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=anie202417779-fig-0002&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fanie.202417779" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                     <p>(a) CV curves of CityU-33 at various scan rates. (b) Determination of b value according to the relationship between peak current and scan rate. (c) Pseudocapacitive and diffusion-controlled storage contributions at different scan rates. (d) Rate performance. (e) Discharge-charge curves of the CityU-33-based electrode at various current densities. (f) Cycle performance of Na//CityU-33 cell at the current density of 0.2 A g<sup>−1</sup>. (g) Comparison of electrochemical performance of Na//CityU-33 cell with representative organic electrode materials.</p></div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>Subsequently, the rate performance at different current densities from 0.1 to 2 A g<sup>−1</sup> was evaluated via a galvanostatic discharge-charge test. As demonstrated in Figure <a href="#anie202417779-fig-0002">2d</a>, the Na//CityU-33 cells display a high discharge capacity of 410.4 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.1 A g<sup>−1</sup>, which is a top performance among all COF electrodes in SIBs. Even at a high current density of 2 A g<sup>−1</sup>, a commendable capacity of 195.1 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> is still achieved, indicating an excellent rate performance. Obviously, such excellent rate performance of Na//CityU-33 cells is largely determined by the fast electrochemical kinetics of the CityU-33 electrode due to the free percolation of Na<sup>+</sup> ions in ordered porosity and the abundance of reversible redox functionalities around channels in CityU-33.<span><sup><a href="#anie202417779-bib-0059" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202417779-bib-0059_R_d242769784e1044" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">22</a></sup></span> Figure <a href="#anie202417779-fig-0002">2e</a> displays the galvanostatic charge/discharge curve of the rate performance, showing similar plateaus at various current densities, further confirming the excellent reversibility and stability of CityU-33. Based on the data presented in Figure <a href="#anie202417779-fig-0002">2d</a>, <a href="#anie202417779-fig-0002">e</a>, the Na//CityU-33 cell shows a maximum energy density of 293.3 Wh kg<sup>−1</sup> at a power density of 149.5 W kg<sup>−1</sup>, calculated based on the total mass of COF cathode and consumed Na anode (see Supporting Information for detailed calculations). The maximum power density of 1620.9 W kg<sup>−1</sup> was achieved with a decent energy density of 162.1 Wh kg<sup>−1</sup> (Figure S13). Moreover, the cycle stability of the Na//CityU-33 cell was also evaluated. When cycled at a current density of 0.2 A g<sup>−1</sup>, the cell achieves a capacity retention of 97 % and a discharge capacity of 288 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> over 2000 cycles (Figure <a href="#anie202417779-fig-0002">2f</a>). Even cycled at a higher current density of 0.5 A g<sup>−1</sup>, the cell still obtains a capacity retention of approximately 90 % over 3000 cycles (Figure S14), indicating the outstanding stability and cyclability of CityU-33. Particularly, the coulombic efficiency of the Na//CityU-33 cell remains very high, approaching 100 % over the entire cycle, further confirming its excellent reversibility. Furthermore, the achieved cycle stability is significantly better than that of previously reported conjugated organic materials (Figure <a href="#anie202417779-fig-0002">2g</a> and Table S1).<span><sup><a href="#anie202417779-bib-0050" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202417779-bib-0050_R_d242769784e1078" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19a - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">19a</a>, <a href="#anie202417779-bib-0051" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202417779-bib-0051_R_d242769784e1078" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19b - Nat. Commun.">19b</a>, <a href="#anie202417779-bib-0053" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202417779-bib-0053_R_d242769784e1078" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19d - Chem. Mater.">19d</a>, <a href="#anie202417779-bib-0056" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202417779-bib-0056_R_d242769784e1078" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20a - J. Mater. Chem. A">20a</a>, <a href="#anie202417779-bib-0074" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202417779-bib-0074_R_d242769784e1078" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 33">33</a></sup></span></p>
         
         <p>The charge storage mechanism of the CityU-33 electrode was first examined using in situ attenuated total reflection Fourier transform infrared (ATR-FTIR) analysis. In this in situ test, the (CV) curve, obtained within a voltage window ranging from 0 V to 3 V (vs. Na/Na<sup>+</sup>) at a scan rate of 0.4 mV s<sup>−1</sup>, was served to illustrate the electrochemical behavior of the CityU-33 electrode (as displayed on the left side of Figure <a href="#anie202417779-fig-0003">3</a>). Correspondingly, the 2D color-filled contour plot of the in situ ATR-FTIR spectra at various potentials (wavenumber vs. time/intensity) are shown on the right side of Figure <a href="#anie202417779-fig-0003">3</a>. The FTIR spectra, collected in the open-circuit state, was used as the reference background. According to the in situ test, the intensities of the peaks at 1150–1250 cm<sup>−1</sup>, 1290–1330 cm<sup>−1</sup>, 1380–1430 cm<sup>−1</sup>, corresponding to the absorption peaks of C−N bond, C−O bond, and C=C bond in the benzene ring, increased during the discharge process. This observation suggests that the C=N and C=O bonds on the nonaromatic ring are gradually transformed into C−N and C−O bonds on aromatic ring during the discharge process. In the subsequent charge process, the absorption peak intensities of C−N, C−O and C=C bonds gradually decreased, indicating high reversibility. Additionally, such periodic variations in the intensity of these characteristic peaks (i.e., C−N, C−O and C=C) are also discernible in subsequent cycles. The ex situ FTIR spectra (Figure S15) in higher frequency area were also collected to provide further insights in the structural changes of CityU-33 at different charge/discharge states. The variations of absorption peaks belonging to C=O, C=N and aromatic C=C in higher frequency area are well agreement with those changes in in situ ATR-FTIR spectra. Besides, the absorption band at ~1509 cm<sup>−1</sup> belonging to C=N in triazine was also periodically changed during charge and discharge processes. All variations substantiate the abundance of redox centers (C=O, C=N, C=C) in CityU-33. The results were also demonstrated by ex situ XPS spectra. As displayed in Figure S16a, the peaks belonging to C=N in N 1s spectra weaken significantly, along with the emerging of the characteristic peak of N−Na (398 eV) after discharged to 0 V. When recharged to 3 V, the strength of the C=N bond restores, and the N−Na bond also disappears, revealing high reversibility. In O 1s XPS spectrum (Figure S16b), the characteristic peak of C=O bond weakens significantly when discharged to 0 V, along with the emerging of a new characteristic peak assigned to O−Na bond. When charged to 3 V, the peak strength of the C=O bond increases with the disappearance of the O−Na bond. The above results confirm that both C=O and C=N bonds are active sites and are responsible for Na<sup>+</sup> storage.
</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="anie202417779-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/97d0c98b-a5f2-47f0-90e5-72c8ce5da4c7/anie202417779-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/97d0c98b-a5f2-47f0-90e5-72c8ce5da4c7/anie202417779-fig-0003-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/0dd98550-058f-41d4-917c-002b2013adc5/anie202417779-fig-0003-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/97d0c98b-a5f2-47f0-90e5-72c8ce5da4c7/anie202417779-fig-0003-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 3<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=anie202417779-fig-0003&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fanie.202417779" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                     <p>The corresponding 2D color filled contour plot in Wavenumber <i>vs</i>. Time of in situ ATR-FTIR test of Na//CityU-33 cell.</p></div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>Density functional theory (DFT) calculations were adopted to evaluate the potential Na<sup>+</sup> storage site within the CityU-33 cell and the corresponding adsorption energies. The molecular electrostatic potential (MESP) was conducted to speculate the active sites for Na<sup>+</sup> storage.<span><sup><a href="#anie202417779-bib-0083" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202417779-bib-0083_R_d242769784e1137" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 34 - J. Comput. Chem.">34</a></sup></span> The MESP of the CityU-33 unit is shown in Figure <a href="#anie202417779-fig-0004">4a</a>, where the oxygen atoms of carbonyl groups, nitrogen atoms of imine groups, triazine units and benzene units with negative charges (blue area) show different degrees of electronegativity, resulting in the electrophilic Na<sup>+</sup> being easily attracted to these sites. The results of MESP are consistent with the conclusions of in situ FTIR spectra and ex situ XPS spectra, indicating that carbonyl (C=O), imine (C=N), triazine (C=N), and benzene rings (C=C) are potential active sites and are responsible for the storage of Na<sup>+</sup>. Additionally, the energy levels of the lowest unoccupied molecular orbital (LUMO) and the highest occupied molecular orbital (HOMO) were calculated to investigate the redox properties of the CityU-33 material. The LUMO (Figure <a href="#anie202417779-fig-0004">4b</a>) and HOMO (Figure S17) energy levels were determined to be −2.85 eV and −5.87 eV, respectively, resulting in a band gap of 3.02 eV. The LUMO orbitals are primarily localized around the highly electronegative sites, such as carbonyl, imine, triazine, and phenyl groups, indicating these regions′ capacity for Na<sup>+</sup> storage. It is worth noting that due to the flexibility of the imine bonds, the CityU-33 repetitive units experienced a degree of distortion during structural optimization. This led to a reduction in the symmetry of the MESP (Figure S18), LUMO (Figure S19), and HOMO (Figure S20). Further calculations were performed to determine the adsorption energy and optimal adsorption structure of Na<sup>+</sup> at each storage site, and the results are shown in Figures S21 to S26. Based on the cyclic voltammetry (CV) curves showing four distinct redox peaks, the charge–discharge process of CityU-33 is divided into four stages (Step 1 to Step 4). The adsorption energies for each step were then calculated according to the sequence of Na<sup>+</sup> insertion, with different amounts of Na<sup>+</sup> inserted at each stage (Figure <a href="#anie202417779-fig-0004">4d</a>). It is noting that the interaction between Na<sup>+</sup> and the carbonyl and imine sites are more likely to undergo interlayer bridging adsorption rather than direct adsorption at a single carbonyl site or a single imine site (Figure S21–S22), because of the AA stacking mode of CityU-33. The total energy of Na<sub>X</sub>-CityU-33 decreases continuously with the embedding of Na<sup>+</sup> in each step (Figure <a href="#anie202417779-fig-0004">4c</a>), indicating that the thermodynamic properties of CityU-33 electrode materials are conducive to the storage of Na<sup>+</sup> during the discharge process. In addition, the crystal volume of CityU-33 remains unchanged during different Na<sup>+</sup> storage processes (Figure S27), indicating that CityU-33 as anode material maintained its structural integrity during Na<sup>+</sup> intercalation and deintercalation, demonstrating excellent cycling stability.
</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="anie202417779-fig-0004"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/b065cbcc-7d91-41dc-b49c-1a1c2f164da4/anie202417779-fig-0004-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/b065cbcc-7d91-41dc-b49c-1a1c2f164da4/anie202417779-fig-0004-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/6f4b08a1-63cc-4eee-a2c1-73b01e88de19/anie202417779-fig-0004-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/b065cbcc-7d91-41dc-b49c-1a1c2f164da4/anie202417779-fig-0004-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 4<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=anie202417779-fig-0004&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fanie.202417779" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                     <p>(a) Schematic diagram of the molecular electrostatic potential (MESP) of the CityU-33 repetitive unit. (b) LUMO plot for per CityU-33 repetitive unit (c) Total energy of unit cells for different Na<sup>+</sup> intercalation step of CityU-33. (d) Top and side views of intercalation structure of n (<i>n</i>=1–12) sodium atoms on each CityU-33 repetitive unit cell.</p></div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="anie202417779-sec-0003">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="anie202417779-sec-0003-title"> Conclusion</h2>
         
         <p>An azatriangulenetrione-contained COF (CityU-33) was synthesized and employed as the anode in SIBs. CityU-33 exhibited excellent performance in SIBs with a high discharge capacity of 410.4 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.1 A g<sup>−1</sup> due to the multiple reachable redox-active sites of C=O, C=N and C=C in its framework. Moreover, owing to the highly ordered structure and uniform nanochannel in CityU-33, the Na//CityU-33 cell exhibited good rate performance with the long cyclability (approximately 90 % preservation at a current density of 0.5 A g<sup>−1</sup> after 3000 cycles). This work provides a strategy to push up the capacity of Na<sup>+</sup> storage by incorporating rich accessible redox-active sites in the backbones of COFs, which could stimulate the ingenious designs of diverse structures of COFs for various applications.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="anie202417779-sec-0004">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="anie202417779-sec-0004-title"> Supporting Information</h2>
         
         <p>The CCDC 2369840 <a href="https://www.ccdc.cam.ac.uk/services/structures?id=doi:10.1002/anie.202417779" class="linkBehavior">https://www.ccdc.cam.ac.uk/services/structures?id=doi:10.1002/anie.202417779</a> for <b>ATTO-3N</b> contain(s) the supplementary crystallographic data for this paper. These data are provided free of charge by the joint Cambridge Crystallographic Data Centre and Fachinformationszentrum Karlsruhe <a href="http://www.ccdc.cam.ac.uk/structures" class="linkBehavior">http://www.ccdc.cam.ac.uk/structures</a> Access Structures service.</p>

        <script type="text/javascript" nonce="eea1629e1cae401bbba43227f93b488d">
    window.addEventListener('load', () => {
        document.querySelectorAll('.admin-bar-popup-menu ul li')?.forEach(function (li) {
            const anchor = li.querySelector('a');
            if (anchor && anchor.textContent.trim() === 'Edit Access Denial') {
                li.style.display = 'none';
            }
        });
